  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d7591a79-3eee-427e-b9dc-3dcc35762306'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer390337455456659051">Peter Carey's Booker Prize winning novel imagines Australia's youth, before its dynamic passions became dangerous habits. It is also a startling and unusual love story. <br/><br/>Oscar is a young English clergyman who has broken with his past and developed a disturbing talent for gambling. A country girl of singular ambition, Lucinda moves to Sydney, driven by dreams of self-relianc</span>
  <span id="freeText390337455456659051" style="display:none">Peter Carey's Booker Prize winning novel imagines Australia's youth, before its dynamic passions became dangerous habits. It is also a startling and unusual love story. <br /><br />Oscar is a young English clergyman who has broken with his past and developed a disturbing talent for gambling. A country girl of singular ambition, Lucinda moves to Sydney, driven by dreams of self-reliance and the building of an industrial Utopia. Together this unlikely pair create and are created by the spectacle of mid-nineteenth century Australia. <br /><br />Peter Carey's visionary brilliance, and his capacity to delight and surprise, propel this story to its stunning conclusion.</span>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2011260452" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2011260452" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2011260452?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=2019-completed">2019-completed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=x-favourites">x-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2011260452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8286446128429561534"><i>
  <b>”In order that I exist, two gamblers, one Obsessive, the other Compulsive, must meet. A door must open at a certain time.”</b>
</i><br/><br/>The narrator is the great-grandson of Oscar Hopkins, and in this passage there followed an explanation of the other criteria that were necessary for Oscar and Lucinda to have met, which ultimately led to the family line surviving through the generations resulting in his own birth. Although the narrator never named himself, once (but only once), someone in his story called</span>
  <span id="freeText8286446128429561534" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>”In order that I exist, two gamblers, one Obsessive, the other Compulsive, must meet. A door must open at a certain time.”</b>
</i><br /><br />The narrator is the great-grandson of Oscar Hopkins, and in this passage there followed an explanation of the other criteria that were necessary for Oscar and Lucinda to have met, which ultimately led to the family line surviving through the generations resulting in his own birth. Although the narrator never named himself, once (but only once), someone in his story called him ‘Bob’. Whether this was a nickname or just an off-the-cuff catch-all name we don’t know. This is because this story isn’t about the narrator and although he tells the story, he effectively keeps himself out of it except for that one tiny and isolated reference.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>The Odd Bod’s face was ghastly, a mask carved out of white soap, and you did not need to be a mind-reader to know that God was sending him to New South Wales. This happened on 22 April 1863. My great-grandfather was twenty-two years old.</b>
</i><br /><br />Odd Bod was the nickname a school chum had given to Oscar Hopkins; initially as an insult but later as an address of some affection. Oscar is a character so naïve in many ways, so pure of mind and heart, that his oddness and strange habits become endearing. This is not a short story – it is a long, involved, sometimes convoluted one – and yet, I wanted more.<br /><br />I grew to care deeply about Oscar and Lucinda as well as many of their friends and acquaintances in the area of Australia known as New South Wales. This story has so many layers of depth and yet it rollicks along with subtle humour and gave cause for me to stop and reflect. Often.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Dennis Hasset looked at the eyes and, knowing how eyes worked, was astonished, not for the first time, at the infinite complexity of Creation, wondering how this thing, this instrument for seeing, could transmit so clearly its entreaty while at the same time – Look, I am only an eye – deny that it was doing anything of the sort.</b>
</i><br /><br />This novel stands out for so many reasons, both blatant and subtle, and yet as I look back on my time spent with this story, it was so tightly and cleverly woven that it is nearly impossible to say “this stands out” or “that stands out”.<br /><br />For me, the entire experience of reading this novel stands out – all of it – the characters, the plot, the writing, stirred in with wit and wisdom, all are like a home-cooked spaghetti sauce: a far more vast and intricate taste sensation than the individual ingredients added to the pot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8286446128429561534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1971826" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1971826" />
    <a title="mark monday" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday"><img alt="mark monday" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572672245p2/131922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1971826?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mark monday" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mark monday" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday">mark monday</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060915926" href="/book/show/2175047.Oscar_and_Lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=romantica">romantica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=mnemonic-devices">mnemonic-devices</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=these-fragile-lives">these-fragile-lives</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=into-the-past">into-the-past</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1971826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14599081405908901608">technicolor and wide-screen in scale and spectacle, quirky and consistently surprising in characterization and incident. virtually a catalog of bizarre imagery, you-are-there historical detail, and way-off-center characters. so many beautiful sequences linger on in the mind, so many wonderful characters, such a surprising lightness of tone, such gorgeous prose... it all almost, but not quite, causes the reader to forget the bleakness at this novel's core. strange, compassionate and, finally, tra</span>
  <span id="freeText14599081405908901608" style="display:none">technicolor and wide-screen in scale and spectacle, quirky and consistently surprising in characterization and incident. virtually a catalog of bizarre imagery, you-are-there historical detail, and way-off-center characters. so many beautiful sequences linger on in the mind, so many wonderful characters, such a surprising lightness of tone, such gorgeous prose... it all almost, but not quite, causes the reader to forget the bleakness at this novel's core. strange, compassionate and, finally, transcendent. oh the beautiful tragedy of it all! <br /><br />there are so many wonderfully odd and evocative parts. "evocative" - an overused word. but it fits this novel. Carey is a generous and intelligent writer, one who does not stint on detail (so much of it at times, some descriptive passages seem to be bursting at the seams) and one who also knows exactly when dry, deadpan dialogue is required. although a tragic story (perhaps), it is also a mordantly amusing one as well. <br /><br />the title characters of Oscar and Lucinda are bold creations. they are endearing despite themselves - Carey does not try to stack the deck by making them too loveable. for me, one of the more absorbing things about their individual characterizations was seeing the difference between an <i> obsessive</i> gambler (Oscar) and a <i>compulsive</i> gambler (Lucinda). i also loved how this novel places gender and race dynamics at the forefront but does not breathe down the reader's neck contstantly with the injustice of it all. crazy Oscar and crazy Lucinda are rebels against society who don't particularly realize their own rebellious nature - they just know that they are somehow <i>different</i>. they don't particularly understand how society puts down blacks, women, and innovative square pegs... but at some level, at times an unconscious one, they recognize injustice and try to fight against it, in their own small ways. they are Beautiful Losers. <br /><br />beautiful novel!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14599081405908901608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_20261104" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20261104" />
    <a title="Kristina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/174245-kristina"><img alt="Kristina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1183759347p2/174245.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20261104?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristina" href="/user/show/174245-kristina">Kristina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who like novels set in the 19th century and unhappy endings</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/174245-kristina?shelf=neo-victorian">neo-victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/174245-kristina?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20261104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11750705115092398355">For the past few years, I've thought about endings a lot. I've excused a lot of novels (esp contemporary ones) for bad or unsatisfying endings. Some novels end in a way that goes against all you've learned from the novel; others just... stop. Then there are the "conservative" endings of Victorian novels that many scholars complain "shut down" or tidy the "subversive" or threatening ideas raised in the novel. Lately I've found myself arguing against this complaint, because even if a novel ends co</span>
  <span id="freeText11750705115092398355" style="display:none">For the past few years, I've thought about endings a lot. I've excused a lot of novels (esp contemporary ones) for bad or unsatisfying endings. Some novels end in a way that goes against all you've learned from the novel; others just... stop. Then there are the "conservative" endings of Victorian novels that many scholars complain "shut down" or tidy the "subversive" or threatening ideas raised in the novel. Lately I've found myself arguing against this complaint, because even if a novel ends conservatively, I don't think that erases what's come before it. After all, you don't read just to get to the end, do you? That's one reason I find it annoying when people complain Victorian novels are "too long" -- are you just reading in order to finish? (One of the students in my Victorian novel class complained that Dickens's BLEAK HOUSE ended at all -- she said, in a wistful way that proclaimed her love for the novel, that she was disappointed because she "thought it would never end.")<br /><br />And then, once in a while, there comes an ending that makes you believe in endings again. Many people here on GoodReads did not like the ending of this novel, which I understand. It is deeply unsettling and unexpected, even though you realize when you get there that the novel sets you up for it for some time. The night I finished reading the novel, I couldn't shake the sadness of the ending and actually woke up in the middle of the night because I was having a bad dream about it. Yes, it was that striking. And that's exactly why I found it to be brilliant. <br /><br />Part of the reason the ending is so good is that the novel simultaneously prepares you for it and upends your expectations. The ending would never be so striking if the novel didn't take its time with the characters -- not just the eccentric title characters, but also the supporting ones. (The word "Dickensian" often gets unfairly slapped on undeserving texts, but what is Dickensian about this novel is the way it approaches each chapter from a distinct perspective and gives you insight into even the most seemingly fleeting and insignificant characters.) If you didn't understand all the feelings, all the convoluted motivations of the characters, the things they reveal to and conceal from each other, then the ending wouldn't have the impact it does. <br /><br />Finally, the ending reveals something that is there throughout but which the reader often forgets: the novel is simultaneously a personal story of two unusual individuals, a story about the lives they touch -- including their progeny, and a story about something much bigger: the very nation those characters inhabit -- the ways they consciously shape it, and the ways they are unknowingly complicit in something they never intended.<br /><br />Plus, Carey writes some gorgeous prose. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11750705115092398355" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2004310134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2004310134" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45632570-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438789125p2/45632570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2004310134?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/45632570-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45632570-tim?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2004310134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6131443272909145251">Well, I can see why Peter Carey has been compared to a contemporary Charles Dickens. His characters and the world he creates have a similar eccentricity and inventiveness and energy. Oscar’s childhood is a sheer delight to read. He’s the son of an overbearing fire and brimstone preacher and marine biologist and there are some memorable images of the two of them on beaches searching for fossils in rock pools. When his wife dies, Oscar’s father takes all her clothes and throw them in the sea. As a</span>
  <span id="freeText6131443272909145251" style="display:none">Well, I can see why Peter Carey has been compared to a contemporary Charles Dickens. His characters and the world he creates have a similar eccentricity and inventiveness and energy. Oscar’s childhood is a sheer delight to read. He’s the son of an overbearing fire and brimstone preacher and marine biologist and there are some memorable images of the two of them on beaches searching for fossils in rock pools. When his wife dies, Oscar’s father takes all her clothes and throw them in the sea. As a result Oscar has a deep seated phobia of salt water. It smells like death to him. At university he’s nicknamed “odd bodd” and discovers he has a mania for gambling, a huge obstacle to his clerical ambitions. <br />Lucinda grows up in a hut in Australia. She’s a kind of pioneer feminist with an obsessive passion for glass. She too is prone to gambling. Like Oscar she has wild hair. She and Oscar meet on a ship bound from the UK to Australia where Oscar is to begin missionary work. What follows is a bittersweet romance of a wholly original nature. They fuse their passions in the idea of building a church made wholly of glass. <br />This novel has it all – a great plot, fantastic character development, brilliant writing – the visual descriptions are consistently wonderful. Absolutely recommended. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6131443272909145251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202287150" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202287150?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/8576612-oscar-and-lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=aere-perennius">aere-perennius</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202287150" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5827248630144567105"><i>“She thought: When we are two, they do not notice us. They think us a match. What wisdom does a mob have? It is a hydra, an organism, stupid or dangerous in much of its behavior, but could it have, in spite of this, a proper judgement about which of its component parts fit best together?”</i> <br/>― Peter Carey, Oscar and Lucinda<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445487106i/16659420._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='259' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>A book to love. A book to wade in, submerge into. A novel that tempts one to grab it around the middle and squeeze, even as it dances away like a shadow. It flickers like the qui</span>
  <span id="freeText5827248630144567105" style="display:none"><i>“She thought: When we are two, they do not notice us. They think us a match. What wisdom does a mob have? It is a hydra, an organism, stupid or dangerous in much of its behavior, but could it have, in spite of this, a proper judgement about which of its component parts fit best together?”</i> <br>― Peter Carey, Oscar and Lucinda<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445487106i/16659420._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="259" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A book to love. A book to wade in, submerge into. A novel that tempts one to grab it around the middle and squeeze, even as it dances away like a shadow. It flickers like the quiet, mirrored Doppler effect of water flowing around a pair of swans. It plays coy. It trips backwards. At times, it really IS too much. But I still love/d it. The prose? Beautiful. The story? Magnificent. Worlds of glass, chance, love, passion, obsession, stars-crossed, God, compulsion, sin, materialism and generosity of spirit. Just like a coin spun/tossed/launched at midday into the sky will twist head over tails -- at once both reflecting and in turn blocking the sun-- this book twists between obsessive Oscar and compulsive Lucinda and spun wildly around a whole slew of characters and just spun there, suspended forever, threatening never to come down. And then it did. And it was glorious.</span>

<div id="review_303234232" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303234232" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6087599-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530478892p2/6087599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/303234232?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/6087599-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679777502" href="/book/show/91112.Oscar_and_Lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer303234232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11470303390132924234">I definitely expected to like this book a lot more than I did, based on another Carey novel I remember loving years ago (<i>True History of the Kelly Gang</i>). The difference, for me, comes down to tone and characterization. While I liked the title characters in <i>Oscar and Lucinda</i> well enough, I wasn't terribly attached to either of them by the end. And something about the tone of this novel I disliked: there's a certain balance (or in my opinion, imbalance) between serious drama/tragedy and comedy/wit</span>
  <span id="freeText11470303390132924234" style="display:none">I definitely expected to like this book a lot more than I did, based on another Carey novel I remember loving years ago (<i>True History of the Kelly Gang</i>). The difference, for me, comes down to tone and characterization. While I liked the title characters in <i>Oscar and Lucinda</i> well enough, I wasn't terribly attached to either of them by the end. And something about the tone of this novel I disliked: there's a certain balance (or in my opinion, imbalance) between serious drama/tragedy and comedy/wit that seems trendy these days, which always rubs me the wrong way. That's not to say that the combination of the two can't be brilliant and genuine, when done with less, well, <i>smugness</i>. That's probably a terribly unfair description, but that's the word that comes to mind, along with jaded, guileful, practiced...etc. <br /><br />The ending, while I appreciate the realism, was a bit too deliberately crafted for shock and effect to resonate with me. It's obviously a polarizing ending, and I can understand why some people would love how different and daring it is, just as others would consider it depressing and unsatisfying. Personally, it just struck me as a too obvious attempt to be edgy. <br /><br />I know I've been pretty negative so far (and maybe TOO negative, due to my own bias towards the tone and style I attempted to describe above) but I actually did enjoy the book for the most part. It's not one I would personally recommend, or ever re-read, but it's interesting enough as a work of historical fiction. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11470303390132924234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64050595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16353080273237910067">How many ways you can tell a love story? How many types of lovers are there in the world? <br/><br/>It tells about the two odd gamblers, <b>Oscar Hopskins,</b> a preacher's son and <b>Lucinda,</b> a heiress who buys a glass factory. The first one is obsessive and the other one is a compulsive gambler. They fell in love on their way to the 19th century Australia. Lucinda challenges Oscar that he cannot move the glass factory to another town and Oscar accepts the challenge and the end is I don't know. What I mean is if i</span>
  <span id="freeText16353080273237910067" style="display:none">How many ways you can tell a love story? How many types of lovers are there in the world? <br /><br />It tells about the two odd gamblers, <b>Oscar Hopskins,</b> a preacher's son and <b>Lucinda,</b> a heiress who buys a glass factory. The first one is obsessive and the other one is a compulsive gambler. They fell in love on their way to the 19th century Australia. Lucinda challenges Oscar that he cannot move the glass factory to another town and Oscar accepts the challenge and the end is I don't know. What I mean is if it justifies the means or if the slow build up (the book is 520 pages and starts when Oscar was a little boy punished by eating Christmas pudding) but earlier fantastic reviews here on Goodreads say that this reflect the way Australia was built as a nation and that the characters juxtaposes Australia and its people and who am I to argue with as the reviewers probably know this better than I do.<br /><br />There are many other memorable scenes. Reading is a bit dragging. Especially when I was done reading the book, I thought that this could be cut in a half and the story would still be the same if not more enjoyable. I have not seen the movie but I thought that given a choice I would like to see how they look like and all the fantastic images in my mind like the inverted cathedral. This is my first time to read a Peter Carey novel but I thought that with those images making prints in my mind, I have no doubt of his skillful artistry in telling stories.<br /><br />I should read more Peter Carey. Thanks to 501 Must Read Books for making me buy this book!<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_15236095" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15236095" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15236095?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679777502" href="/book/show/91112.Oscar_and_Lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=australian-fiction">australian-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15236095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7485792927728687884">no spoilers; just synopsis<br/><br/>a) don't see the movie unless you read the book...something gets really lost between the two<br/><br/>b)Excellent, simply excellent!!! I would recommend this book to anyone who appreciates superlative writing and a quirky story. If every book were like this one, I would be in Heaven!!!! The prose is outstanding and these characters are simply so real I thought they'd float off the page.<br/><br/>Oscar and Lucinda is set both in England and in Australia in the 19th century. In England, Osca</span>
  <span id="freeText7485792927728687884" style="display:none">no spoilers; just synopsis<br /><br />a) don't see the movie unless you read the book...something gets really lost between the two<br /><br />b)Excellent, simply excellent!!! I would recommend this book to anyone who appreciates superlative writing and a quirky story. If every book were like this one, I would be in Heaven!!!! The prose is outstanding and these characters are simply so real I thought they'd float off the page.<br /><br />Oscar and Lucinda is set both in England and in Australia in the 19th century. In England, Oscar Hopkins is the son of a non-Anglican, religious fundamentalist who is also a naturalist, and up until he is about 15 Oscar grows up with the reassurance that he is among the saved. Oscar's mother died; he lives with his father in a little village called Hennacombe in Devon, in an austere house with no ornamentation; even the food is plain. One Christmas one of the cooks feels sorry for the boy and makes him a Christmas pudding, complete with raisins &amp; a cherry; the ostentatiousness of the pudding leads Theophilus (Oscar's father) to lose it and he hits Oscar, who is then forced to cough up the pudding. Later, they are out wading in the ocean, and Oscar asks that God smite his father out of anger; just then, Theophilus has an accident that cuts him on the leg. Oscar realizes that he has to leave -- and the signs point to the Anglican Church. We next find him at Oxford, at Oriel College, where he discovers gambling. One thing leads to another and Oscar sets out to become a missionary in New South Wales but he has to go by ship...a problem since Oscar has this immense water phobia. It is on this voyage that Oscar meets Lucinda Leplastrier, returning to Australia, whose parents had died &amp; whose mother, before dying, had their land subdivided and sold and Lucinda was now an heiress living off the profits. She is also the owner of a glassworks in Australia. Lucinda is obstinate, headstrong &amp; like Oscar, she is a gambler. The lives of these two people come together on the ship, then meet again after Oscar discovers that there is no Missionary Work to be done in New South Wales, and that he is to be assigned to a posh vicarage instead. He meets Lucinda in a Chinese gambling house ... and things take off from there. I won't say another word... you really should read it for yourself.<br /><br />The writing is excellent; the story is excellent and there are so many themes that are explored without the author ever losing track. My only complaint: the end came so fast (it was a great ending but rushed) that after having savored the story for so long I felt cheated. However, the rest of the book was absolutely stunning and so rich so I can overlook this.<br /><br />Please try this book...I can totally see how it won a Booker.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7485792927728687884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21900117" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21900117" />
    <a title="Trevor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/124789-trevor"><img alt="Trevor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343945834p2/124789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21900117?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor" href="/user/show/124789-trevor">Trevor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571180950" href="/book/show/2240033.Oscar_and_Lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/124789-trevor?shelf=1988">1988</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21900117" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9703352982633745743">When I started this book I knew I was in for something different. Two gamblers fall in love and conspire to transport a glass church across the outback in colonial times? And it's good? Yes, it is good. <br/><br/>Peter Carey's Oscar and Lucinda was a great trip for me. I loved being immersed in the details of the 1850s and 1860s. I especially loved being immersed in the details of the mind from this period. This is not a simple love story. The characters feel deeply about many things, and have many differ</span>
  <span id="freeText9703352982633745743" style="display:none">When I started this book I knew I was in for something different. Two gamblers fall in love and conspire to transport a glass church across the outback in colonial times? And it's good? Yes, it is good. <br /><br />Peter Carey's Oscar and Lucinda was a great trip for me. I loved being immersed in the details of the 1850s and 1860s. I especially loved being immersed in the details of the mind from this period. This is not a simple love story. The characters feel deeply about many things, and have many different feelings: guilt, pleasure, holiness, despair, longing, loneliness. The list could go on. It's a rich rich book. <br /><br />While the basic story is what's written above, it is more honest to say that the transporting a glass church thing is just something that happens in the book. It was a way for Carey to really dig into deep questions of the soul--faith, doubt, righteousness, hypocrisy, wickedness, the fragility of relationships. While I read this book I really cared for the characters as they struggled to find their identity amidst so much external and internal conflict.<br /><br />The book is also pretty funny. There are several great parts that made me laugh out loud. It's the way Carey describes things and his sense of timing. But that same talent helps him also achieve a devestating effect in the reader. The comical story is real, but so is the tragedy and the despair. The relationships are so important to the characters, who have struggled so hard to connect with anyone, yet the relationships, while honest, are also desperate. It's hard to read at times for fear of what discoveries will be made.<br /><br />The books started slowly, though not unpleasantly. We see Oscar and Lucinda growing up, experiencing their rather harsh childhoods. Oscar is a bit strange to others. Lucinda too, only its because she's a rather strong willed woman. Both recognize their situation, and even though they recognize that they should not worry because they feel they are being exactly who they should be, it still hurts. On a side note, almost, they each become addicted to gambling. Ultimately they gamble everything they care for on each other. This all somehow leads to a very insightful look at faith, colonialism, love, death.<br /><br />Somehow in this book Carey has taken a million minute, seemingly unimportant details, and compiled them into something touching and important. I like the way one of the Best of the Booker judges described it as building the Taj Mahal out of matchsticks (or something along those lines).</span>
  <a data-text-id="9703352982633745743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dax" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dax" href="/user/show/72907701-dax">Dax</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679777502" href="/book/show/91112.Oscar_and_Lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72907701-dax?shelf=booker-prize">booker-prize</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2300127725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15950606406425272331">I can understand why Peter Carey is not for everyone. His novels tend to move slowly with a focus on subtlety. I find his work to be, much like the sentences he composes, charming. In "Oscar and Lucinda" we find subdued humor and understated actions that possess significant implications. Some might find this quiet approach boring, but I have a soft spot for novels that don't like to reveal too much at a time. <br/><br/>In this particular novel, Carey does a masterful job of portraying the awkwardness of c</span>
  <span id="freeText15950606406425272331" style="display:none">I can understand why Peter Carey is not for everyone. His novels tend to move slowly with a focus on subtlety. I find his work to be, much like the sentences he composes, charming. In "Oscar and Lucinda" we find subdued humor and understated actions that possess significant implications. Some might find this quiet approach boring, but I have a soft spot for novels that don't like to reveal too much at a time. <br /><br />In this particular novel, Carey does a masterful job of portraying the awkwardness of courtship. Oscar and Lucinda are constantly misreading each other and it makes for a realistic account of the early stages of relationships. Carey is clearly having a lot of fun with this aspect of the story.<br /><br />But this is not just a love story. This is a novel about the role of chance in our lives and how accidents can play a significant role in the formation of our perceptions. The ending, which did not unfold how I expected it to in any way, hammers that point home.<br /><br />There's a lot to love about this one, but I can understand those who find it a little too tedious. "Oscar and Lucinda" confirmed my appreciation for the author.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3984775612398639795">“Writing long books is a laborious and impoverishing act of foolishness: expanding in five hundred pages an idea that could be perfectly explained in a few minutes" Jorge Luis Borges<br/><br/><br/>“So the writer who breeds more words than he needs, is making a chore for the reader who reads.”<br/>― Dr. Seuss<br/><br/>What a pity. There hasn't been a book that has annoyed me as much as this one. I can't take this prose style anymore. It talks about 2 "outcasts", I couldn't find a plausible reason other than their own assumpti</span>
  <span id="freeText3984775612398639795" style="display:none">“Writing long books is a laborious and impoverishing act of foolishness: expanding in five hundred pages an idea that could be perfectly explained in a few minutes" Jorge Luis Borges<br /><br /><br />“So the writer who breeds more words than he needs, is making a chore for the reader who reads.”<br />― Dr. Seuss<br /><br />What a pity. There hasn't been a book that has annoyed me as much as this one. I can't take this prose style anymore. It talks about 2 "outcasts", I couldn't find a plausible reason other than their own assumptions of them being outcast. Lucinda keeps reminding that she is woman and is not accepted in business circle each time she surfaces in the novel. Oscar, I don't know! After 270 pages I still failed to like a single character, failed to understand what was so important in those 270 pages, don't understand what could be so stunning about lovestory between 2 hyped up characters, don't know what historical or cultural significance the novel could present what it couldn't till now! Whatever I read in 270 pages could have very well written off in 2-3 pages. I don't regret of stopping this despite of not getting a whiff of the love story between the main characters, which was supposed to be the idea behind this whole novel!<br /></span>

<div id="review_2871031785" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2871031785" />
    <a title="Lucy Banks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57486568-lucy-banks"><img alt="Lucy Banks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483536933p2/57486568.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2871031785?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucy Banks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucy Banks" href="/user/show/57486568-lucy-banks">Lucy Banks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2871031785" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18324808305858519347"><b>Loved the characters and setting, a really intricate, fascinating book.</b><br/><br/>I seem to be on a bit of a roll with my Australian authors at the moment - and I must confess, Peter Carey (despite being mega famous) was new to me. <br/><br/>I had zero concept of what the book would be about - perhaps I was expecting some sort of love story, set in the Australian outback? However, surprisingly I got a red-headed gambler, a wealthy Australian heiress, a glass factory, and lots more besides.<br/><br/><b>Plot</b><br/><br/>Oscar lives in Devon (U</span>
  <span id="freeText18324808305858519347" style="display:none"><b>Loved the characters and setting, a really intricate, fascinating book.</b><br /><br />I seem to be on a bit of a roll with my Australian authors at the moment - and I must confess, Peter Carey (despite being mega famous) was new to me. <br /><br />I had zero concept of what the book would be about - perhaps I was expecting some sort of love story, set in the Australian outback? However, surprisingly I got a red-headed gambler, a wealthy Australian heiress, a glass factory, and lots more besides.<br /><br /><b>Plot</b><br /><br />Oscar lives in Devon (UK) with his father. He gets a good education, becomes a clergyman, then travels to Australia. On the boat, he meets Lucinda, a wealthy woman in possession of a glass factory. They share a mutual passion - for gambling. However, this is a society that doesn't permit women to gamble, and it's not fond of men of the cloth doing it either. <br /><br />Their gambling hides a far deeper connection, but one that is also marred by the expectations of society, and the people around them. Oscar is ruined (partly due to Lucinda's actions), and she hires him in her glass factory. Their shared love of glass, combined with their reckless need to gamble, means they both take a wild risk with their fortunes... and not for the better. <br /><br /><b>My review</b><br /><br />Peter Carey is another of those authors who perfectly captures the heat and hostility of the Australian landscape, combined with the personality of the place - the prosaic, sweeping elegance of it all, combined with the coarseness, and the struggle to survive. <br /><br />Oscar and Lucinda are both unsuited to this challenging environment. I love how the author showed their vulnerability through their clandestine gambling sessions, Lucinda's crumbling factory, and Oscar's faltering career as a parson. They, like the Australian landscape, are being forced to comply with societal expectations, and both of them fail miserably. <br /><br />The ending was a bit overblown (though I liked to sense of 'complete collapse' and self-annihilation), which is why I didn't rate it a 5* - it was gorgeously subtle up to that point, and I wanted it to stay that way. However, I loved the idea of the cracking glass-church - what an image! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18324808305858519347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/21938800-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21938800-john?shelf=man-booker-prize-winners">man-booker-prize-winners</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2870498300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8485643918425977406">A great novel. Two eccentric characters are thrown together with all the flaws multiples sevenfold. Oscar brought up by his father a Plymouth Brethren living in a remote village in Devon He develops odd habits and beliefs. He then decides to become Anglican and later a clergyman Oxford who supports his studies by gambling. There is a transition via the Reverend Stratton who supports his change of belief. <br/><br/>On the other side of the world Lucinda becomes an heiress after her father and later her mot</span>
  <span id="freeText8485643918425977406" style="display:none">A great novel. Two eccentric characters are thrown together with all the flaws multiples sevenfold. Oscar brought up by his father a Plymouth Brethren living in a remote village in Devon He develops odd habits and beliefs. He then decides to become Anglican and later a clergyman Oxford who supports his studies by gambling. There is a transition via the Reverend Stratton who supports his change of belief. <br /><br />On the other side of the world Lucinda becomes an heiress after her father and later her mother dies. She also is a gambler. One compulsive the other obsessive. Lucinda buys a glassworks and then travels to England. Returning to Australia on the Leviathan she meets Oscar. Later in Australia she allows Oscar to move in with her after Oscar is kicked out of his church due to his gambling. <br /><br />Then the two of them make a bet which changes their lives forever. The journey to fulfill the bet allows Oscar to see the reality of Australian landscape and the people. Evil, debauchery, drunkenness and results in a murder and Oscar making some extremely bad decisions. I liked the ending and somehow it seemed apt. Well worth a read an entertaining and in places funny book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8485643918425977406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3034161158" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3034161158" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33119635-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544045847p2/33119635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3034161158?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/33119635-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23266934-oscar-and-lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33119635-neil?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3034161158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15978827821480380275">I picked this book up at a charity book shop a few weeks ago. It’s hard to say no to a Booker winner for £1, even if it is 30+ years old.<br/><br/>I found this a confusing book to read. About one-third of the way through I made a note that said “this seems to have suddenly become a different book”. It changed tone and became much darker, but it seemed to do it in the turn of a single page. And then it changed again toward the end and it goes to some very dark places in its final sections. This is all noti</span>
  <span id="freeText15978827821480380275" style="display:none">I picked this book up at a charity book shop a few weeks ago. It’s hard to say no to a Booker winner for £1, even if it is 30+ years old.<br /><br />I found this a confusing book to read. About one-third of the way through I made a note that said “this seems to have suddenly become a different book”. It changed tone and became much darker, but it seemed to do it in the turn of a single page. And then it changed again toward the end and it goes to some very dark places in its final sections. This is all noticeable because the book starts off very light-hearted. If the move to darkness had been more gradual, I would probably be praising it for the way it drew me in. But as it is, it felt like it completely changed at a couple of significant points and that felt quite jarring. Perhaps this was intentional by the author.<br /><br />The novel tells the unconventional love story of Oscar Hopkins and Lucinda Leplastrier. It’s an unusual love story because they spend most of the book unaware of one another and on completely the opposite sides of the world. This confused me for a while because I read a summary before I started that talked about them both being gambling addicts and making a wager. This does happen, but not until a good three-quarters of the book is in your left hand.<br /><br />I was further confused by the mention of red-backed hawks in Devon, UK (to the best of my knowledge, we’ve never had those here) and by the talk of “electric megaphones” in use in 1865 when the Internet tells me these were invented in 1878.<br /><br />So, for a variety of reasons, some of my own making, I spent a lot of this book in a confused state.<br /><br />It is a historical novel set in Great Britain and Australia in the mid-late nineteenth century. Oscar Hopkins is the son of a Plymouth Brethren preacher who is often reminiscent of The Dice Man in the way he determines God’s will for his life by chance. It is chance that leads him to “convert” to Anglicanism and then on to Australia. But his commitment to chance also expresses itself in his obsessive gambling. His path crosses with that of Lucinda Leplastrier. Lucinda is a feminist ahead of her time who spends a large proportion of her significant inheritance on a glassworks in Sydney where she encounters major resistance to the idea of a woman being in business. But Lucinda’s life is also governed by a compulsion to gamble and it is this that draws the pair together. It is also the wager between the two of them that leads the novel to the darkness with which it ends.<br /><br />The book is filled with arresting images. Perhaps the most memorable is that of a glass church floating on a raft down a river through the Australian bush. Large parts of this section of the book brought back memories of the movie Fitcarraldo where a man transports a steamship over a steep hill in the Amazon. It’s a fast moving story: in 511 pages it squeezes in 110 chapters and the story rattles along at the kind of pace that suggests.<br /><br />For the most part, I enjoyed reading the book (apart from my self-induced confusion about some bits of it). But I never felt all that excited about it. Others have written about not being able to put it down even though it gives you chance to do that every 2 or 3 pages with the brief chapters. For me, I found that I was taking rather more of the opportunities it presented for a break than I would normally. I’m not quite sure why I didn’t fully engage with it. Maybe I should watch the movie.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15978827821480380275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer557478511793158903">It's such a while since I read this book but it's right up there among my all time favourites. <br/><br/>Oscar and Lucinda are such improbable characters ... Unfit for the world on so many levels but with robust conviction in their own world view. While they should both be cowering forlornly in some remote and dimly lit place, they embark on a mad mission with the sort of passion we all hope to experience at least once in our lives but probably never will. <br/><br/>The Prince Rupert's Drop that so impresses the yo</span>
  <span id="freeText557478511793158903" style="display:none">It's such a while since I read this book but it's right up there among my all time favourites. <br /><br />Oscar and Lucinda are such improbable characters ... Unfit for the world on so many levels but with robust conviction in their own world view. While they should both be cowering forlornly in some remote and dimly lit place, they embark on a mad mission with the sort of passion we all hope to experience at least once in our lives but probably never will. <br /><br />The Prince Rupert's Drop that so impresses the young Lucinda symbolically represents the incredible resilience and terrifying vulnerability that reside within us all and is so beautifully illustrated by the two misfit heroes of this book. <br /><br />I love and I hate that Carey lied to me (or at the very least deliberately tricked me) and I went scrambling back through the book to find the point of deception <br /><br />It's not the characters that make this book so thoroughly Australian, it's the way Carey tells the tale. It's there in the trickery, the irreverence and the affectionate contempt the narrator tells the tale. You can almost smell the eucalyptus</span>

<div id="review_1598960852" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1598960852" />
    <a title="Stef Rozitis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31302654-stef-rozitis"><img alt="Stef Rozitis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528589606p2/31302654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1598960852?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stef Rozitis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stef Rozitis" href="/user/show/31302654-stef-rozitis">Stef Rozitis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571244171" href="/book/show/3483648-oscar-and-lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31302654-stef-rozitis?shelf=intersectionality">intersectionality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31302654-stef-rozitis?shelf=recommended-to-me">recommended-to-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31302654-stef-rozitis?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31302654-stef-rozitis?shelf=australian">australian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31302654-stef-rozitis?shelf=1981-1990">1981-1990</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1598960852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16727097961743772621">So many conflicting feelings. The book is so exquisitely written and worked, the characters quite believable and Carey has a rare talent for writing believable and deeply explored female characters although male himself. The book is so tragic, unfolds into layers and layers of ever bleaker despair but with touches of humanity that make you long for joy.<br/><br/>Is it a true story? It could be true. The thin blue line between greatness and madness is walked for the whole 500 pages. The chapters are blesse</span>
  <span id="freeText16727097961743772621" style="display:none">So many conflicting feelings. The book is so exquisitely written and worked, the characters quite believable and Carey has a rare talent for writing believable and deeply explored female characters although male himself. The book is so tragic, unfolds into layers and layers of ever bleaker despair but with touches of humanity that make you long for joy.<br /><br />Is it a true story? It could be true. The thin blue line between greatness and madness is walked for the whole 500 pages. The chapters are blessedly short and the point of view shifts in a way that is completely fair on the reader. The worlds explored are so masculine, so harsh, so violent! Real love attempts to flower again and again and a thousand circumstances and the weakness of various characters stops it growing to fruition.<br /><br />I feel a bit depressed about the worldview in the book, I always feel that with Carey. How can he paint beauty into these grey, harsh landscapes of despair? The crafting of it is masterful, the whole thing drips with human weakness that is convincing. But so bleak!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16727097961743772621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18381267800860961214" style="display:none">Although I had heard of <i>Parrot and Olivier in America</i>, I wasn’t really familiar with Peter Carey before I ran across Warwick’s review of <i>Illywhacker </i> which you can find <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/803582829?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1&quot;" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. <div id="review_306929607" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306929607" />
    <a title="Julie Tridle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2146556-julie-tridle"><img alt="Julie Tridle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519677759p2/2146556.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/306929607?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Tridle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Tridle" href="/user/show/2146556-julie-tridle">Julie Tridle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer306929607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6014757614867661228">I've considered giving this book a fifth star. The writing was pretty much perfect, the story unique and the characters interesting and memorable. I also know I've given five stars to books that weren't nearly as well-written. I suspect I'm being stingy with that final star because the book didn't keep me riveted. It's not really a book you read to find out what will happen next but to take in and savor what you are reading now, and I'm not sure it's fair to punish a book for that. Still, I will</span>
  <span id="freeText6014757614867661228" style="display:none">I've considered giving this book a fifth star. The writing was pretty much perfect, the story unique and the characters interesting and memorable. I also know I've given five stars to books that weren't nearly as well-written. I suspect I'm being stingy with that final star because the book didn't keep me riveted. It's not really a book you read to find out what will happen next but to take in and savor what you are reading now, and I'm not sure it's fair to punish a book for that. Still, I will keep my final star. I tend to only give it to books I love so much that I hate to see it end. This wasn't one of those books for me, but it is still an excellent read. <br /><br /><br />Had to come back and give this one its fifth star. I think about it too much since finishing it and find I use it too much as a standard for judging other books I read to leave it as a four star-er.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6014757614867661228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_352955084" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/352955084" />
    <a title="Chaitra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9086236-chaitra"><img alt="Chaitra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432611617p2/9086236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/352955084?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chaitra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chaitra" href="/user/show/9086236-chaitra">Chaitra</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679777502" href="/book/show/91112.Oscar_and_Lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=made-into-a-movie">made-into-a-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=booker-prize-winners">booker-prize-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=saga">saga</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer352955084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13811055491961959727">I began watching the movie they made of this book, but didn't complete it. From what I remembered, it seemed to be a story about two gamblers, brought together by a fantastical wager - to transport a glass church across unmapped Australian bush. Or so I thought. And it is, except it is also mostly not. <br/><br/>The glass church is the unraveling of this meticulously crafted book. Up until then I enjoyed it. The characterization of Oscar and Lucinda is exceptional. I did not like either of them, not one l</span>
  <span id="freeText13811055491961959727" style="display:none">I began watching the movie they made of this book, but didn't complete it. From what I remembered, it seemed to be a story about two gamblers, brought together by a fantastical wager - to transport a glass church across unmapped Australian bush. Or so I thought. And it is, except it is also mostly not. <br /><br />The glass church is the unraveling of this meticulously crafted book. Up until then I enjoyed it. The characterization of Oscar and Lucinda is exceptional. I did not like either of them, not one little bit, but still understood them, and wanted to know what happened to them. Oscar's obsession and Lucinda's compulsion was very keenly observed. The gender politics of the time; while Lucinda herself was hard to like, it was hard not to be upset by her treatment by the men who surrounded her. None of the rest of the cast escapes being flawed, but they are all very well written.<br /><br />I was with the story up until the glass church. Even after the wager is made, I still enjoyed some parts of it - Mr. Ahearn calling Lucinda out on its failings, for example. It was so head scratching to me, the fact that they would build a church on the principles of a glasshouse and not think of the heat, that I would be upset had no one called them out on it. But the last 50 pages had me shaking my head and thinking, 'well that got out of hand quite quickly'. I don't mind what happened in those pages, but I think it too out there. It might probably be the point, but it jarred me out of what I felt I was reading. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It's not Oscar's dying, but of him dying and Lucinda losing her glassworks at the same time, and by his hand. To a gold digger. It's a bit too neat, in its misfortune <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />I had only read one other Peter Carey book, which I didn't particularly like, and I am so-so on Australian fiction in general, but this book makes me want to seek out more of Carey's work. It's not a book I loved without reservations. But it had so much that was good, even within the parts that I did not care for. I don't feel that about most books. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13811055491961959727" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1580158922" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1580158922" />
    <a title="Emilia Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32845594-emilia-barnes"><img alt="Emilia Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404673186p2/32845594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1580158922?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilia Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilia Barnes" href="/user/show/32845594-emilia-barnes">Emilia Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571270163" href="/book/show/10356549-oscar-and-lucinda">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=genre-historical">genre-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=period-victorian">period-victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=genre-classic">genre-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=hero-beta">hero-beta</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32845594-emilia-barnes?shelf=rated-will-read-again">rated-will-read-again</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1580158922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12765859728469016902">An impressive book, to say the least. Oscar and Lucinda isn't like any other historical novel I ever read. It's mind-boggling in its richness, and the level of detail Carey manages. I don't even mean historical detail (which is pitched really well to cast you right in the time and place) but the fictional detail (the complexity of people's lives, and I mean side characters here). Carey forgets about nobody. Sometimes it just became too much for me (hence 4 stars in the end). It is a witty, extra</span>
  <span id="freeText12765859728469016902" style="display:none">An impressive book, to say the least. Oscar and Lucinda isn't like any other historical novel I ever read. It's mind-boggling in its richness, and the level of detail Carey manages. I don't even mean historical detail (which is pitched really well to cast you right in the time and place) but the fictional detail (the complexity of people's lives, and I mean side characters here). Carey forgets about nobody. Sometimes it just became too much for me (hence 4 stars in the end). It is a witty, extraordinarily well-crafted, beautiful novel, which I might read again, just to fully absorb everything now that I won't be trying to race to the end to see how it all finishes. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12765859728469016902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
